<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Coral reef</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

<p><b>Coral reefs</b> are diverse underwater <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystems" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a> held together by <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> structures secreted by <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">corals</a>. Coral reefs are built by <a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)" title="Colony (biology)">colonies</a> of tiny animals found in <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">marine water</a> that contain few nutrients. Most coral reefs are built from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stony_coral" title="Stony coral">stony corals</a>, which in turn consist of <a href="/wiki/Polyp" title="Polyp">polyps</a> that cluster in groups. The polyps belong to a group of animals known as <a href="/wiki/Cnidaria" title="Cnidaria">Cnidaria</a>, which also includes <a href="/wiki/Sea_anemone" title="Sea anemone">sea anemones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a>. Unlike sea anemones, corals secrete hard carbonate <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">exoskeletons</a> which support and protect the coral polyps. Most reefs grow best in warm, shallow, clear, sunny and agitated water.</p>
<p>Often called "rainforests of the sea", shallow coral reefs form some of the most diverse ecosystems on Earth. They occupy less than 0.1% of the world's ocean surface, about half the area of France, yet they provide a home for at least 25% of all marine <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spalding_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spalding-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mulhall_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mulhall-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusk" title="Mollusk">mollusks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Worm" title="Worm">worms</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crustaceans" title="Crustaceans">crustaceans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">echinoderms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">sponges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunicate" title="Tunicate">tunicates</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cnidarians" title="Cnidarians">cnidarians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="#Darwin.27s_paradox">Paradoxically</a>, coral reefs flourish even though they are surrounded by ocean waters that provide few nutrients. They are most commonly found at shallow depths in tropical waters, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_water_coral" title="Deep water coral">deep water</a> and cold water corals also exist on smaller scales in other areas.</p>
<p>Coral reefs deliver <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a> to tourism, fisheries and <a href="/wiki/Coastal_management" title="Coastal management">shoreline protection</a>. The annual global economic value of coral reefs is estimated between US$30–375 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cesar_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cesar-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Costanza_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Costanza-7">[7]</a></sup> However, coral reefs are fragile ecosystems, partly because they are very sensitive to water temperature. They are under threat from <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">climate change</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oceanic_acidification" title="Oceanic acidification">oceanic acidification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blast_fishing" title="Blast fishing">blast fishing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyanide_fishing" title="Cyanide fishing">cyanide fishing</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquarium_fish" title="Aquarium fish">aquarium fish</a>, sunscreen use,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sunscreen_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sunscreen-8">[8]</a></sup> overuse of reef resources, and harmful land-use practices, including urban and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agricultural_runoff" title="Agricultural runoff">agricultural runoff</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>, which can harm reefs by encouraging excess <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algal</a> growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corals_reveal_impact_of_land_use_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Corals_reveal_impact_of_land_use-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fringing_reef" title="Fringing reef">Fringing reef</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">Atoll</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Structure_and_Distribution_of_Coral_Reefs" title="The Structure and Distribution of Coral Reefs">The Structure and Distribution of Coral Reefs</a></div>
<p>Most of the coral reefs we can see today were formed after the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a> when melting ice caused the <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a> to rise and flood the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelves</a>. This means that most modern coral reefs are less than 10,000 years old. As communities established themselves on the shelves, the reefs grew upwards, pacing rising <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea levels</a>. Reefs that rose too slowly could become drowned reefs. They are covered by so much water that there was insufficient light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Coral reefs are found in the deep sea away from <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelves</a>, around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oceanic_island" title="Oceanic island">oceanic islands</a> and as <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">atolls</a>. The vast majority of these islands are <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic</a> in origin. The few exceptions have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic" title="Tectonic">tectonic</a> origins where <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate movements</a> have lifted the deep ocean floor on the surface.</p>
<p>In 1842 in his first <a href="/wiki/Monograph" title="Monograph">monograph</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Structure_and_Distribution_of_Coral_Reefs" title="The Structure and Distribution of Coral Reefs">The Structure and Distribution of Coral Reefs</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> set out his theory of the formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atoll_reef" title="Atoll reef">atoll reefs</a>, an idea he conceived during the <a href="/wiki/Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle" title="Second voyage of HMS Beagle">voyage of the <i>Beagle</i></a>. He theorized <a href="/wiki/Tectonic_uplift" title="Tectonic uplift">uplift</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subsidence" title="Subsidence">subsidence</a> of the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_crust" title="Oceanic crust">crust</a> under the oceans formed the atolls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cr_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-cr-14">[14]</a></sup> Darwin’s theory sets out a sequence of three stages in atoll formation. It starts with a <a href="/wiki/Fringing_reef" title="Fringing reef">fringing reef</a> forming around an extinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanic_island" title="Volcanic island">volcanic island</a> as the island and ocean floor subsides. As the subsidence continues, the fringing reef becomes a barrier reef, and ultimately an atoll reef.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atoll_forming-volcano.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="194" data-file-width="263" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Atoll_forming-volcano.png/109px-Atoll_forming-volcano.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Atoll_forming-volcano.png/163px-Atoll_forming-volcano.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Atoll_forming-volcano.png/217px-Atoll_forming-volcano.png 2x" width="109"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Darwin’s theory starts with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanic_island" title="Volcanic island">volcanic island</a> which becomes extinct</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:28.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atoll_forming-Fringing_reef.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="117" data-file-width="264" height="53" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Atoll_forming-Fringing_reef.png/120px-Atoll_forming-Fringing_reef.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Atoll_forming-Fringing_reef.png/180px-Atoll_forming-Fringing_reef.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Atoll_forming-Fringing_reef.png/240px-Atoll_forming-Fringing_reef.png 2x" width="120"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>As the island and ocean floor subside, coral growth builds a <a href="/wiki/Fringing_reef" title="Fringing reef">fringing reef</a>, often including a shallow lagoon between the land and the main reef.</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:31.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atoll_forming-Barrier_reef.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="102" data-file-width="263" height="47" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Atoll_forming-Barrier_reef.png/120px-Atoll_forming-Barrier_reef.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Atoll_forming-Barrier_reef.png/180px-Atoll_forming-Barrier_reef.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Atoll_forming-Barrier_reef.png/240px-Atoll_forming-Barrier_reef.png 2x" width="120"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>As the subsidence continues, the fringing reef becomes a larger barrier reef further from the shore with a bigger and deeper <a href="/wiki/Lagoon" title="Lagoon">lagoon</a> inside.</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:33.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atoll_forming-Atoll.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="95" data-file-width="263" height="43" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Atoll_forming-Atoll.png/120px-Atoll_forming-Atoll.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Atoll_forming-Atoll.png/180px-Atoll_forming-Atoll.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Atoll_forming-Atoll.png/240px-Atoll_forming-Atoll.png 2x" width="120"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Ultimately, the island sinks below the sea, and the barrier reef becomes an <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">atoll</a> enclosing an open lagoon.</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Darwin predicted that underneath each <a href="/wiki/Lagoon" title="Lagoon">lagoon</a> would be a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bed_rock" title="Bed rock">bed rock</a> base, the remains of the original volcano. Subsequent drilling proved this correct. Darwin's theory followed from his understanding that coral polyps thrive in the clean seas of the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> where the water is agitated, but can only live within a limited depth range, starting just below low <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tide</a>. Where the level of the underlying earth allows, the corals grow around the coast to form what he called fringing reefs, and can eventually grow out from the shore to become a barrier reef.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coral_atoll_formation_animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="237" data-file-width="320" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Coral_atoll_formation_animation.gif/220px-Coral_atoll_formation_animation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d8/Coral_atoll_formation_animation.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coral_atoll_formation_animation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A fringing reef can take ten thousand years to form, and an atoll can take up to 30 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Where the bottom is rising, fringing reefs can grow around the coast, but coral raised above sea level dies and becomes white <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>. If the land subsides slowly, the fringing reefs keep pace by growing upwards on a base of older, dead coral, forming a barrier reef enclosing a lagoon between the reef and the land. A barrier reef can encircle an island, and once the island sinks below sea level a roughly circular atoll of growing coral continues to keep up with the sea level, forming a central lagoon. Barrier reefs and atolls do not usually form complete circles, but are broken in places by storms. Like sea level rise, a rapidly subsiding bottom can overwhelm coral growth, killing the coral polyps and the reef, due to what is called <i>coral drowning</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster_Coral_subsidence_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_Coral_subsidence-16">[16]</a></sup> Corals that rely on <a href="/wiki/Zooxanthellae" title="Zooxanthellae">zooxanthellae</a> can <i>drown</i> when the water becomes too deep for their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbionts" title="Symbionts">symbionts</a> to adequately <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesize</a>, due to decreased light exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster_coral_drowning_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_coral_drowning-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The two main variables determining the <a href="/wiki/Geomorphology" title="Geomorphology">geomorphology</a>, or shape, of coral reefs are the nature of the underlying <a href="/wiki/Substrate_(biology)" title="Substrate (biology)">substrate</a> on which they rest, and the history of the change in sea level relative to that substrate.</p>
<p>The approximately 20,000-year-old <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Great Barrier Reef">Great Barrier Reef</a> offers an example of how coral reefs formed on continental shelves. Sea level was then 120 m (390 ft) lower than in the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBRMPAage_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-GBRMPAage-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIMSage_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-AIMSage-19">[19]</a></sup> As sea level rose, the water and the corals encroached on what had been hills of the Australian coastal plain. By 13,000 years ago, sea level had risen to 60 m (200 ft) lower than at present, and many hills of the coastal plains had become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_islands" title="Continental islands">continental islands</a>. As the sea level rise continued, water topped most of the continental islands. The corals could then overgrow the hills, forming the present <a href="/wiki/Cay" title="Cay">cays</a> and reefs. Sea level on the Great Barrier Reef has not changed significantly in the last 6,000 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIMSage_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-AIMSage-19">[19]</a></sup> and the age of the modern living reef structure is estimated to be between 6,000 and 8,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCage_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRCage-20">[20]</a></sup> Although the Great Barrier Reef formed along a continental shelf, and not around a volcanic island, Darwin's principles apply. Development stopped at the barrier reef stage, since Australia is not about to submerge. It formed the world's largest barrier reef, 300–1,000 m (980–3,280 ft) from shore, stretching for 2,000 km (1,200 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Healthy tropical coral reefs grow horizontally from 1 to 3 cm (0.39 to 1.18 in) per year, and grow vertically anywhere from 1 to 25 cm (0.39 to 9.84 in) per year; however, they grow only at depths shallower than 150 m (490 ft) because of their need for sunlight, and cannot grow above sea level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Materials">Materials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Materials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As the name implies, the bulk of coral reefs is made up of coral skeletons from mostly intact coral colonies. As other chemical elements present in corals become incorporated into the calcium carbonate deposits, <a href="/wiki/Aragonite" title="Aragonite">aragonite</a> is formed. However, shell fragments and the remains of calcareous algae such as the green-segmented <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Halimeda" title="Halimeda">Halimeda</a></i> can add to the reef's ability to withstand damage from storms and other threats. Such mixtures are visible in structures such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eniwetok_Atoll" title="Eniwetok Atoll">Eniwetok Atoll</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murph_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-murph-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The three principal reef types are:</p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Fringing_reef" title="Fringing reef">Fringing reef</a></b> – directly attached to a shore, or borders it with an intervening shallow channel or lagoon</li>
<li><b>Barrier reef</b> – reef separated from a mainland or island shore by a deep channel or <a href="/wiki/Lagoon" title="Lagoon">lagoon</a></li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atoll_reef" title="Atoll reef">Atoll reef</a></b> – more or less circular or continuous barrier reef extends all the way around a lagoon without a central island</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maldives_small_island.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1066" data-file-width="1600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Maldives_small_island.jpg/220px-Maldives_small_island.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Maldives_small_island.jpg/330px-Maldives_small_island.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Maldives_small_island.jpg/440px-Maldives_small_island.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maldives_small_island.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A small <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">atoll</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maldives_-_Kurumba_Island.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Maldives_-_Kurumba_Island.jpg/220px-Maldives_-_Kurumba_Island.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Maldives_-_Kurumba_Island.jpg/330px-Maldives_-_Kurumba_Island.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Maldives_-_Kurumba_Island.jpg/440px-Maldives_-_Kurumba_Island.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maldives_-_Kurumba_Island.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Inhabited <a href="/wiki/Cay" title="Cay">cay</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Other reef types or variants are:</p>
<ul>
<li><b>Patch reef</b> – common, isolated, comparatively small reef outcrop, usually within a <a href="/wiki/Lagoon" title="Lagoon">lagoon</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embayment" title="Embayment">embayment</a>, often circular and surrounded by sand or seagrass</li>
<li><b>Apron reef</b> – short reef resembling a fringing reef, but more sloped; extending out and downward from a point or peninsular shore</li>
<li><b>Bank reef</b> – linear or semicircular shaped-outline, larger than a patch reef</li>
<li><b>Ribbon reef</b> – long, narrow, possibly winding reef, usually associated with an atoll lagoon</li>
<li><b>Table reef</b> – isolated reef, approaching an atoll type, but without a lagoon</li>
<li><b>Habili</b> – reef specific to the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>; does not reach the surface near enough to cause visible <a href="/wiki/Breaking_wave" title="Breaking wave">surf</a>; may be a hazard to ships (from the <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> for "unborn")</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Microatoll" title="Microatoll">Microatoll</a></b> – community of species of corals; vertical growth limited by average tidal height; growth morphologies offer a low-resolution record of patterns of sea level change; fossilized remains can be dated using <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">radioactive carbon dating</a> and have been used to reconstruct <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea levels</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smithers_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smithers-24">[24]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Cay" title="Cay">Cays</a></b> – small, low-elevation, sandy islands formed on the surface of coral reefs from eroded material that piles up, forming an area above sea level; can be stabilized by plants to become habitable; occur in tropical environments throughout the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Oceans</a> (including the Caribbean and on the <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Great Barrier Reef">Great Barrier Reef</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belize_Barrier_Reef" title="Belize Barrier Reef">Belize Barrier Reef</a>), where they provide habitable and agricultural land</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Seamount" title="Seamount">Seamount</a></b> or <b><a href="/wiki/Guyot" title="Guyot">guyot</a></b> – formed when a coral reef on a volcanic island subsides; tops of seamounts are rounded and guyots are flat; flat tops of guyots, or <i>tablemounts</i>, are due to erosion by waves, winds, and atmospheric processes</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Zones">Zones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Zones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coral_reef_diagram.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="326" data-file-width="491" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Coral_reef_diagram.jpg/300px-Coral_reef_diagram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Coral_reef_diagram.jpg/450px-Coral_reef_diagram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/11/Coral_reef_diagram.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coral_reef_diagram.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The three major zones of a coral reef: the fore reef, reef crest, and the back reef</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Coral reef ecosystems contain distinct zones that represent different kinds of habitats. Usually, three major zones are recognized: the fore reef, reef crest, and the back reef (frequently referred to as the reef lagoon).</p>
<p>All three zones are physically and ecologically interconnected. Reef life and oceanic processes create opportunities for exchange of <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediments</a>, nutrients, and marine life among one another.</p>
<p>Thus, they are integrated components of the coral reef ecosystem, each playing a role in the support of the reefs' abundant and diverse fish assemblages.</p>
<p>Most coral reefs exist in shallow waters less than 50 m deep. Some inhabit tropical continental shelves where cool, nutrient rich <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a> does not occur, such as <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Great Barrier Reef">Great Barrier Reef</a>. Others are found in the deep ocean surrounding islands or as <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">atolls</a>, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a>. The reefs surrounding islands form when islands subside into the ocean, and atolls form when an island subsides below the surface of the sea.</p>
<p>Alternatively, Moyle and Cech distinguish six zones, though most reefs possess only some of the zones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoyleCech556_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-MoyleCech556-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Propagation_du_tsunami_en_profondeur_variable.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="225" data-file-width="429" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Propagation_du_tsunami_en_profondeur_variable.gif/220px-Propagation_du_tsunami_en_profondeur_variable.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Propagation_du_tsunami_en_profondeur_variable.gif/330px-Propagation_du_tsunami_en_profondeur_variable.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2d/Propagation_du_tsunami_en_profondeur_variable.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Propagation_du_tsunami_en_profondeur_variable.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Water in the reef surface zone is often agitated. This diagram represents a reef on a <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a>. The water waves at the left travel over the <i>off-reef floor</i> until they encounter the <i>reef slope</i> or <i>fore reef</i>. Then the waves pass over the shallow <i>reef crest</i>. When a wave enters shallow water it <a href="/wiki/Wave_shoaling" title="Wave shoaling">shoals</a>, that is, it slows down and the wave height increases.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>The reef surface</b> is the shallowest part of the reef. It is subject to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_surge" title="Tidal surge">surge</a> and the rise and fall of <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a>. When waves pass over shallow areas, they <a href="/wiki/Wave_shoaling" title="Wave shoaling">shoal</a>, as shown in the diagram at the right. This means the water is often agitated. These are the precise condition under which corals flourish. Shallowness means there is plenty of light for <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> by the symbiotic <a href="/wiki/Zooxanthellae" title="Zooxanthellae">zooxanthellae</a>, and agitated water promotes the ability of coral to feed on <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a>. However, other organisms must be able to withstand the robust conditions to flourish in this zone.</p>
<p><b>The off-reef floor</b> is the shallow sea floor surrounding a reef. This zone occurs by reefs on continental shelves. Reefs around tropical islands and atolls drop abruptly to great depths, and do not have a floor. Usually sandy, the floor often supports <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seagrass_meadow" title="Seagrass meadow">seagrass meadows</a> which are important foraging areas for reef fish.</p>
<p><b>The reef drop-off</b> is, for its first 50 m, habitat for many reef fish who find shelter on the cliff face and <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a> in the water nearby. The drop-off zone applies mainly to the reefs surrounding oceanic islands and atolls.</p>
<p><b>The reef face</b> is the zone above the reef floor or the reef drop-off. This zone is often the most diverse area of the reef. Coral and <a href="/wiki/Calcareous" title="Calcareous">calcareous</a> algae growths provide complex habitats and areas which offer protection, such as cracks and crevices. Invertebrates and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epiphytic" title="Epiphytic">epiphytic</a> algae provide much of the food for other organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoyleCech556_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-MoyleCech556-25">[25]</a></sup> A common feature on this forereef zone is <a href="/wiki/Spur_and_groove_formation" title="Spur and groove formation">spur and groove formations</a> which serve to transport sediment downslope.</p>
<p><b>The reef flat</b> is the sandy-bottomed flat, which can be behind the main reef, containing chunks of coral. This zone may border a lagoon and serve as a protective area, or it may lie between the reef and the shore, and in this case is a flat, rocky area. Fishes tend to prefer living in that flat, rocky area, compared to any other zone, when it is present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoyleCech556_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-MoyleCech556-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>The reef lagoon</b> is an entirely enclosed region, which creates an area less affected by wave action that often contains small reef patches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoyleCech556_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-MoyleCech556-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, the "topography of coral reefs is constantly changing. Each reef is made up of irregular patches of algae, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sessility_(zoology)" title="Sessility (zoology)">sessile</a> invertebrates, and bare rock and sand. The size, shape and relative abundance of these patches changes from year to year in response to the various factors that favor one type of patch over another. Growing coral, for example, produces constant change in the fine structure of reefs. On a larger scale, tropical storms may knock out large sections of reef and cause boulders on sandy areas to move."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Locations">Locations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Locations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coral_reef_locations.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="224" data-file-width="640" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Coral_reef_locations.jpg/300px-Coral_reef_locations.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Coral_reef_locations.jpg/450px-Coral_reef_locations.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Coral_reef_locations.jpg/600px-Coral_reef_locations.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coral_reef_locations.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Locations of coral reefs</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20_Grad_Isotherme.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1404" data-file-width="2759" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/20_Grad_Isotherme.png/300px-20_Grad_Isotherme.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/20_Grad_Isotherme.png/450px-20_Grad_Isotherme.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/20_Grad_Isotherme.png/600px-20_Grad_Isotherme.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

Boundary for 20 °C <a href="/wiki/Contour_line" title="Contour line">isotherms</a>. Most corals live within this boundary. Note the cooler waters caused by upwelling on the southwest coast of Africa and off the coast of Peru.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Upwelling_image1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="337" data-file-width="514" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Upwelling_image1.jpg/300px-Upwelling_image1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Upwelling_image1.jpg/450px-Upwelling_image1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Upwelling_image1.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Upwelling_image1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This map shows areas of <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a> in red. Coral reefs are not found in coastal areas where colder and nutrient-rich upwellings occur.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raja_Ampat_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="1800" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Raja_Ampat_2.jpg/300px-Raja_Ampat_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Raja_Ampat_2.jpg/450px-Raja_Ampat_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Raja_Ampat_2.jpg/600px-Raja_Ampat_2.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raja_Ampat_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Marine biodiversity of <a href="/wiki/Raja_Ampat_Islands" title="Raja Ampat Islands">Raja Ampat Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>. It is estimated that about 75% of world coral reef population are in the Raja Ampat Islands</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_reefs" title="List of reefs">List of reefs</a></div>
<p>Coral reefs are estimated to cover 284,300 km<sup>2</sup> (109,800 sq mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> just under 0.1% of the oceans' surface area. The <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pacific" title="Indo-Pacific">Indo-Pacific</a> region (including the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific" title="Pacific">Pacific</a>) account for 91.9% of this total. Southeast Asia accounts for 32.3% of that figure, while the Pacific including <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> accounts for 40.8%. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic" title="Atlantic">Atlantic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> coral reefs account for 7.6%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spalding_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spalding-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although corals exist both in temperate and tropical waters, shallow-water reefs form only in a zone extending from approximately 30° N to 30° S of the equator. Tropical corals do not grow at depths of over 50 meters (160 ft). The optimum temperature for most coral reefs is 26–27 °C (79–81 °F), and few reefs exist in waters below 18 °C (64 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> However, reefs in the Persian Gulf have adapted to temperatures of 13 °C (55 °F) in winter and 38 °C (100 °F) in summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenpeace_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenpeace-29">[29]</a></sup> There are 37 species of scleractinian corals identified in such harsh environment around Larak Island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Deep-water_coral" title="Deep-water coral">Deep-water coral</a> can exist at greater depths and colder temperatures at much higher latitudes, as far north as Norway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Although deep water corals can form reefs, very little is known about them.</p>
<p>Coral reefs are rare along the west coasts of the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, due primarily to <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a> and strong cold coastal currents that reduce water temperatures in these areas (respectively the Peru, Benguela and Canary streams).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nybakken_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nybakken-32">[32]</a></sup> Corals are seldom found along the coastline of <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>—from the eastern tip of India (<a href="/wiki/Chennai" title="Chennai">Chennai</a>) to the <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> borders<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spalding_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Spalding-2">[2]</a></sup>—as well as along the coasts of northeastern <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> and Bangladesh, due to the freshwater release from the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River" title="Amazon River">Amazon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a> Rivers respectively.</p>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Great Barrier Reef">Great Barrier Reef</a>—largest, comprising over 2,900 individual reefs and 900 islands stretching for over 2,600 kilometers (1,600 mi) off <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland, Australia</a></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_Barrier_Reef_System" title="Mesoamerican Barrier Reef System">Mesoamerican Barrier Reef System</a>—second largest, stretching 1,000 kilometers (620 mi) from <a href="/wiki/Isla_Contoy" title="Isla Contoy">Isla Contoy</a> at the tip of the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a> down to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bay_Islands_(department)" title="Bay Islands (department)">Bay Islands of Honduras</a></li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Caledonia_Barrier_Reef" title="New Caledonia Barrier Reef">New Caledonia Barrier Reef</a>—second longest double barrier reef, covering 1,500 kilometers (930 mi)</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Andros,_Bahamas" title="Andros, Bahamas">Andros, Bahamas</a> Barrier Reef—third largest, following the east coast of Andros Island, Bahamas, between <a href="/wiki/Andros" title="Andros">Andros</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nassau,_Bahamas" title="Nassau, Bahamas">Nassau</a></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>—includes 6000-year-old fringing reefs located around a 2,000 km (1,240 mi) coastline</li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florida_Reef_Tract" title="Florida Reef Tract">Florida Reef Tract</a>—largest continental US reef and the third largest coral barrier reef system in the world, extends from Soldier Key, located in Biscayne Bay, to the Dry Tortugas in the Gulf of Mexico<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pulley_Ridge" title="Pulley Ridge">Pulley Ridge</a>—deepest photosynthetic coral reef, <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a></li>
<li>Numerous reefs scattered over the <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> coral reef area, the second largest in Southeast Asia, is estimated at 26,000 square kilometers and holds an extraordinary diversity of species. Scientists have identified 915 reef fish species and more than 400 scleractinian coral species, 12 of which are endemic.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Raja_Ampat_Islands" title="Raja Ampat Islands">Raja Ampat Islands</a> in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>'s <a href="/wiki/West_Papua_(province)" title="West Papua (province)">West Papua</a> province offer the highest known marine diversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a> is known for its northernmost coral reef system, located at 32.4° N and 64.8° W. The presence of coral reefs at this high latitude is due to the proximity of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a>. Bermuda has a fairly consistent diversity of coral species, representing a subset of those found in the greater Caribbean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li>The world's northernmost individual coral reef so far discovered is located within a bay of Japan's <a href="/wiki/Tsushima_Island" title="Tsushima Island">Tsushima Island</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Korea_Strait" title="Korea Strait">Korea Strait</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></li>
<li>The world's southernmost coral reef is at <a href="/wiki/Lord_Howe_Island" title="Lord Howe Island">Lord Howe Island</a>, in the Pacific Ocean off the east coast of Australia.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coral_polyp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="670" data-file-width="462" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Coral_polyp.jpg/220px-Coral_polyp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Coral_polyp.jpg/330px-Coral_polyp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Coral_polyp.jpg/440px-Coral_polyp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coral_polyp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Anatomy of a <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">coral</a> <a href="/wiki/Polyp" title="Polyp">polyp</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">Coral</a></div>
<p>Alive corals are <a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)" title="Colony (biology)">colonies</a> of small animals embedded in <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> shells. It is a mistake to think of coral as plants or rocks. Coral heads consist of accumulations of individual animals called <a href="/wiki/Polyp" title="Polyp">polyps</a>, arranged in diverse shapes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Polyps are usually tiny, but they can range in size from a pinhead to 12 inches (30 cm) across.</p>
<p>Reef-building or <a href="/wiki/Hermatypic_coral" title="Hermatypic coral">hermatypic corals</a> live only in the <a href="/wiki/Photic_zone" title="Photic zone">photic zone</a> (above 50 m), the depth to which sufficient sunlight penetrates the water, allowing photosynthesis to occur. Coral polyps do not photosynthesize, but have a symbiotic relationship with microscopic <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Symbiodinium" title="Symbiodinium">Symbiodinium</a></i>, commonly referred to as zooxanthellae. These organisms live within the tissues of polyps and provide organic nutrients that nourish the polyp. Because of this relationship, coral reefs grow much faster in clear water, which admits more sunlight. Without their symbionts, coral growth would be too slow to form significant reef structures. Corals get up to 90% of their nutrients from their symbionts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuideCoralBleaching_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-GuideCoralBleaching-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reefs grow as polyps and other organisms deposit calcium carbonate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> the basis of coral, as a skeletal structure beneath and around themselves, pushing the coral head's top upwards and outwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Waves, grazing fish (such as <a href="/wiki/Parrotfish" title="Parrotfish">parrotfish</a>), <a href="/wiki/Sea_urchin" title="Sea urchin">sea urchins</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_sponge" title="Sea sponge">sponges</a>, and other forces and organisms act as <a href="/wiki/Bioerosion" title="Bioerosion">bioeroders</a>, breaking down coral skeletons into fragments that settle into spaces in the reef structure or form sandy bottoms in associated reef lagoons. Many other organisms living in the reef community contribute skeletal calcium carbonate in the same manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coralline_algae" title="Coralline algae">Coralline algae</a> are important contributors to reef structure in those parts of the reef subjected to the greatest forces by waves (such as the reef front facing the open ocean). These algae strengthen the reef structure by depositing limestone in sheets over the reef surface.</p>
<p>Typical shapes for coral <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> are <a href="/wiki/Brain_coral" title="Brain coral">wrinkled brains</a>, cabbages, <a href="/wiki/Acropora" title="Acropora">table tops</a>, <a href="/wiki/Staghorn_coral" title="Staghorn coral">antlers</a>, wire strands and <a href="/wiki/Pillar_coral" title="Pillar coral">pillars</a>. These shapes can depend on the life history of the coral, like light exposure and wave action,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> and events such as breakages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FFS_Table_bottom.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="440" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/FFS_Table_bottom.jpg/180px-FFS_Table_bottom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/FFS_Table_bottom.jpg/270px-FFS_Table_bottom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/FFS_Table_bottom.jpg/360px-FFS_Table_bottom.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FFS_Table_bottom.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Acropora" title="Acropora">Table coral</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coral_detail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Coral_detail.jpg/180px-Coral_detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Coral_detail.jpg/270px-Coral_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Coral_detail.jpg/360px-Coral_detail.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coral_detail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Close up of polyps are arrayed on a coral, waving their tentacles. There can be thousands of polyps on a single coral branch.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Corals reproduce both sexually and asexually. An individual polyp uses both reproductive modes within its lifetime. Corals reproduce sexually by either internal or external fertilization. The reproductive cells are found on the <a href="/wiki/Mesentery_(zoology)" title="Mesentery (zoology)">mesenteries</a>, membranes that radiate inward from the layer of tissue that lines the stomach cavity. Some mature adult corals are hermaphroditic; others are exclusively male or female. A few <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> change sex as they grow.</p>
<p>Internally fertilized eggs develop in the polyp for a period ranging from days to weeks. Subsequent development produces a tiny <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larva</a>, known as a <a href="/wiki/Planula" title="Planula">planula</a>. Externally fertilized eggs develop during synchronized spawning. Polyps release eggs and sperm into the water en masse, simultaneously. Eggs disperse over a large area. The timing of spawning depends on time of year, water temperature, and tidal and lunar cycles. Spawning is most successful when there is little variation between high and low <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tide</a>. The less water movement, the better the chance for fertilization. Ideal timing occurs in the spring. Release of eggs or planula usually occurs at night, and is sometimes in phase with the lunar cycle (three to six days after a full moon). The period from release to settlement lasts only a few days, but some planulae can survive afloat for several weeks. They are vulnerable to predation and environmental conditions. The lucky few planulae which successfully attach to substrate next confront competition for food and space.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>There are eight <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clades</a> of Symbiodinium <a href="/wiki/Phylotype" title="Phylotype">phylotypes</a>. Most research has been completed on the Symbiodinium clades A–D. Each one of the eight contributes their own benefits as well as less compatible attributes to the survival of their coral hosts. Each photosynthetic organism has a specific level of sensitivity to photodamage of compounds needed for survival, such as proteins. Rates of regeneration and replication determine the organism's ability to survive. Phylotype A is found more in the shallow regions of marine waters. It is able to produce mycosporine-like <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> that are UV resistant, using a derivative of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycerin" title="Glycerin">glycerin</a> to absorb the UV radiation and allowing them to become more receptive to warmer water temperatures. In the event of UV or thermal damage, if and when repair occurs, it will increase the likelihood of survival of the host and symbiont. This leads to the idea that, evolutionarily, clade A is more UV resistant and thermally resistant than the other clades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Clades B and C are found more frequently in the deeper water regions, which may explain the higher susceptibility to increased temperatures. Terrestrial plants that receive less sunlight because they are found in the undergrowth can be analogized to clades B, C, and D. Since clades B through D are found at deeper depths, they require an elevated light absorption rate to be able to synthesize as much energy. With elevated absorption rates at UV wavelengths, the deeper occurring phylotypes are more prone to coral bleaching versus the more shallow clades. Clade D has been observed to be high temperature-tolerant, and as a result it has a higher rate of survival than clades B and C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-45">[45]</a></sup></p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 81.333333333333px">
