<h2><span id="Darwin's_paradox"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Darwin.27s_paradox">Darwin's paradox</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Darwin's paradox">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #ffffff; width:260px;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<center><b>Darwin's paradox</b></center>
"Coral... seems to proliferate when ocean waters are warm, poor, clear and agitated, a fact which Darwin had already noted when he passed through Tahiti in 1842. This constitutes a fundamental paradox, shown quantitatively by the apparent impossibility of balancing input and output of the nutritive elements which control the coral polyp metabolism.
<p>Recent oceanographic research has brought to light the reality of this paradox by confirming that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oligotrophy" title="Oligotrophy">oligotrophy</a> of the ocean <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euphotic" title="Euphotic">euphotic</a> zone persists right up to the swell-battered reef crest. When you approach the reef edges and atolls from the quasidesert of the open sea, the near absence of living matter suddenly becomes a plethora of life, without transition. So why is there something rather than nothing, and more precisely, where do the necessary nutrients for the functioning of this extraordinary coral reef machine come from?"</p>
— Francis Rougerie<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Structure_and_Distribution_of_Coral_Reefs" title="The Structure and Distribution of Coral Reefs">The Structure and Distribution of Coral Reefs</a></i>, published in 1842, Darwin described how coral reefs were found in some areas of the tropical seas but not others, with no obvious cause. The largest and strongest corals grew in parts of the reef exposed to the most violent surf and corals were weakened or absent where loose sediment accumulated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_structure_and_distribution_of_coral_reefs_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_structure_and_distribution_of_coral_reefs-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tropical waters contain few nutrients<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> yet a coral reef can flourish like an "oasis in the desert".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> This has given rise to the ecosystem conundrum, sometimes called "Darwin's paradox": "How can such high production flourish in such nutrient poor conditions?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goeij2009_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goeij2009-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coral reefs cover less than 0.1% of the surface of the world’s ocean, about half the land area of France, yet they support over one-quarter of all marine species. This diversity results in complex <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">food webs</a>, with large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator_fish" title="Predator fish">predator fish</a> eating smaller <a href="/wiki/Forage_fish" title="Forage fish">forage fish</a> that eat yet smaller <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> and so on. However, all food webs eventually depend on <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, which are the <a href="/wiki/Primary_producers" title="Primary producers">primary producers</a>. Coral reefs' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_productivity" title="Primary productivity">primary productivity</a> is very high, typically producing 5–10 grams of carbon per square meter per day (gC·m<sup>−2</sup>·day<sup>−1</sup>) <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">biomass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>One reason for the unusual clarity of tropical waters is they are deficient in nutrients and drifting <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a>. Further, the sun shines year-round in the tropics, warming the surface layer, making it less dense than subsurface layers. The warmer water is separated from deeper, cooler water by a stable <a href="/wiki/Thermocline" title="Thermocline">thermocline</a>, where the temperature makes a rapid change. This keeps the warm surface waters floating above the cooler deeper waters. In most parts of the ocean, there is little exchange between these layers. Organisms that die in aquatic environments generally sink to the bottom, where they decompose, which releases nutrients in the form of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> (N), <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> (P) and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> (K). These nutrients are necessary for plant growth, but in the tropics, they do not directly return to the surface.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Plants form the base of the food chain, and need sunlight and nutrients to grow. In the ocean, these plants are mainly microscopic <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> which drift in the <a href="/wiki/Water_column" title="Water column">water column</a>. They need sunlight for <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, which powers <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">carbon fixation</a>, so they are found only relatively near the surface. But they also need nutrients. Phytoplankton rapidly use nutrients in the surface waters, and in the tropics, these nutrients are not usually replaced because of the <a href="/wiki/Thermocline" title="Thermocline">thermocline</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polyps_(PSF).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1212" data-file-width="1308" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Polyps_%28PSF%29.png/220px-Polyps_%28PSF%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Polyps_%28PSF%29.png/330px-Polyps_%28PSF%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Polyps_%28PSF%29.png/440px-Polyps_%28PSF%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polyps_(PSF).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coral polyps</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Explanations">Explanations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Explanations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Around coral reefs, lagoons fill in with material eroded from the reef and the island. They become havens for marine life, providing protection from waves and storms.</p>
<p>Most importantly, reefs <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">recycle</a> nutrients, which happens much less in the open ocean. In coral reefs and lagoons, producers include phytoplankton, as well as seaweed and coralline algae, especially small types called turf algae, which pass nutrients to corals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castro-56">[56]</a></sup> The phytoplankton are eaten by fish and crustaceans, who also pass nutrients along the food web. Recycling ensures fewer nutrients are needed overall to support the community.</p>
<p>Coral reefs support many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbiotic" title="Symbiotic">symbiotic</a> relationships. In particular, <a href="/wiki/Zooxanthellae" title="Zooxanthellae">zooxanthellae</a> provide energy to coral in the form of <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amino_acids" title="Amino acids">amino acids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Zooxanthellae can provide up to 90% of a coral’s energy requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuideCoralBleaching_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-GuideCoralBleaching-38">[38]</a></sup> In return, as an example of <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualism</a>, the corals shelter the zooxanthellae, averaging one million for every cubic centimeter of coral, and provide a constant supply of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> they need for photosynthesis.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Multy_color_corals.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1224" data-file-width="1632" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Multy_color_corals.JPG/220px-Multy_color_corals.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Multy_color_corals.JPG/330px-Multy_color_corals.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Multy_color_corals.JPG/440px-Multy_color_corals.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Multy_color_corals.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The color of corals depends on the combination of brown shades provided by their <a href="/wiki/Zooxanthellae" title="Zooxanthellae">zooxanthellae</a> and pigmented proteins (reds, blues, greens, etc.) produced by the corals themselves.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Corals also absorb nutrients, including inorganic nitrogen and phosphorus, directly from water. Many corals extend their tentacles at night to catch <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> that brush them when the water is agitated. Zooplankton provide the polyp with nitrogen, and the polyp shares some of the nitrogen with the zooxanthellae, which also require this element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Castro-56">[56]</a></sup> The varying pigments in different species of zooxanthellae give them an overall brown or golden-brown appearance, and give brown corals their colors. Other pigments such as reds, blues, greens, etc. come from colored proteins made by the coral animals. Coral which loses a large fraction of its zooxanthellae becomes white (or sometimes pastel shades in corals that are richly pigmented with their own colorful proteins) and is said to be <a href="/wiki/Coral_bleaching" title="Coral bleaching">bleached</a>, a condition which, unless corrected, can kill the coral.</p>
<p>Sponges are another key: they live in crevices in the coral reefs. They are efficient <a href="/wiki/Filter_feeder" title="Filter feeder">filter feeders</a>, and in the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> they consume about 60% of the phytoplankton that drifts by. The sponges eventually excrete nutrients in a form the corals can use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sea_Cotton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Sea_Cotton.jpg/220px-Sea_Cotton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Sea_Cotton.jpg/330px-Sea_Cotton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Sea_Cotton.jpg/440px-Sea_Cotton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sea_Cotton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Most coral polyps are nocturnal feeders. Here, in the dark, polyps have extended their tentacles to feed on zooplankton.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The roughness of coral surfaces is the key to coral survival in agitated waters. Normally, a boundary layer of still water surrounds a submerged object, which acts as a barrier. Waves breaking on the extremely rough edges of corals disrupt the boundary layer, allowing the corals access to passing nutrients. Turbulent water thereby promotes reef growth and branching. Without the nutritional gains brought by rough coral surfaces, even the most effective recycling would leave corals wanting in nutrients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Studies have shown that deep nutrient-rich water entering coral reefs through isolated events may have significant effects on temperature and nutrient systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leichter_et_al._1996_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leichter_et_al._1996-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolanski_and_Pickard_1983_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolanski_and_Pickard_1983-61">[61]</a></sup> This water movement disrupts the relatively stable thermocline that usually exists between warm shallow water to deeper colder water. Leichter et al. (2006)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> found that temperature regimes on coral reefs in the Bahamas and Florida were highly variable with temporal scales of minutes to seasons and spatial scales across depths.</p>
<p>Water can be moved through coral reefs in various ways, including current rings, surface waves, internal waves and tidal changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leichter_et_al._1996_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leichter_et_al._1996-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ezer_et_al._2011_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ezer_et_al._2011-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fratantoni_and_Richardson_2006_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fratantoni_and_Richardson_2006-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leichter_et_al._1998_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leichter_et_al._1998-65">[65]</a></sup> Movement is generally created by tides and wind. As tides interact with varying bathymetry and wind mixes with surface water, internal waves are created. An internal wave is a gravity wave that moves along density stratification within the ocean. When a water parcel encounters a different density it will oscillate and create internal waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talley_et_al._2011_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Talley_et_al._2011-66">[66]</a></sup> While internal waves generally have a lower frequency than surface waves, they often form as a single wave that breaks into multiple waves as it hits a slope and moves upward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helfrich_1992_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Helfrich_1992-67">[67]</a></sup> This vertical break up of internal waves causes significant diapycnal mixing and turbulence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gregg_1989_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gregg_1989-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_1992_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor_1992-69">[69]</a></sup> Internal waves can act as nutrient pumps, bringing plankton and cool nutrient-rich water up to the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leichter_et_al._1996_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-Leichter_et_al._1996-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leichter_et_al._1998_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leichter_et_al._1998-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andrews_and_Gentien_1982_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andrews_and_Gentien_1982-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandstrom_and_Elliott_1984_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sandstrom_and_Elliott_1984-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolanski_and_Hamner_1988_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolanski_and_Hamner_1988-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rougerie_et_al._1992_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rougerie_et_al._1992-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolanski_and_Delesalle_1993_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolanski_and_Delesalle_1993-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Szmant_and_Forrester_1996_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Szmant_and_Forrester_1996-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furnas_and_Mitchell_1996_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Furnas_and_Mitchell_1996-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leichter_and_Miller_1999_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leichter_and_Miller_1999-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leichter_et_al._2003_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leichter_et_al._2003-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>The irregular structure characteristic of coral reef bathymetry may enhance mixing and produce pockets of cooler water and variable nutrient content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leichter_et_al._2005_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leichter_et_al._2005-79">[79]</a></sup> Arrival of cool, nutrient-rich water from depths due to internal waves and tidal bores has been linked to growth rates of suspension feeders and benthic algae<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leichter_et_al._1998_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-Leichter_et_al._1998-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leichter_et_al._2003_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leichter_et_al._2003-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_et_al._2004_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_et_al._2004-80">[80]</a></sup> as well as plankton and larval organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leichter_et_al._1998_65-3"><a href="#cite_note-Leichter_et_al._1998-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pineda_1994_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pineda_1994-81">[81]</a></sup> Leichter et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leichter_et_al._2003_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-Leichter_et_al._2003-78">[78]</a></sup> proposed that the seaweed <a href="/wiki/Codium" title="Codium"><i>Codium isthmocladum</i></a> reacts to deep water nutrient sources due to their tissues having different concentrations of nutrients dependent upon depth. Wolanski and Hamner<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolanski_and_Hamner_1988_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wolanski_and_Hamner_1988-72">[72]</a></sup> noted aggregations of eggs, larval organisms and plankton on reefs in response to deep water intrusions. Similarly, as internal waves and bores move vertically, surface-dwelling larval organisms are carried toward the shore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pineda_1994_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pineda_1994-81">[81]</a></sup> This has significant biological importance to cascading effects of food chains in coral reef ecosystems and may provide yet another key to unlocking "Darwin's Paradox".</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">Cyanobacteria</a> provide soluble <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrates</a> for the reef via <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen fixation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coral reefs also often depend on surrounding habitats, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seagrass_meadow" title="Seagrass meadow">seagrass meadows</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mangrove_forest" title="Mangrove forest">mangrove forests</a>, for nutrients. Seagrass and mangroves supply dead plants and animals which are rich in nitrogen and also serve to feed fish and animals from the reef by supplying wood and vegetation. Reefs, in turn, protect mangroves and seagrass from waves and produce <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a> in which the mangroves and seagrass can root.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenpeace_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenpeace-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Callyspongia_sp._(Tube_sponge).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="1800" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Callyspongia_sp._%28Tube_sponge%29.jpg/220px-Callyspongia_sp._%28Tube_sponge%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Callyspongia_sp._%28Tube_sponge%29.jpg/330px-Callyspongia_sp._%28Tube_sponge%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Callyspongia_sp._%28Tube_sponge%29.jpg/440px-Callyspongia_sp._%28Tube_sponge%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Tube sponges attracting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardinal_fish" title="Cardinal fish">cardinal fishes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asiatic_glassfish" title="Asiatic glassfish">glassfishes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wrasse" title="Wrasse">wrasses</a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Underwater_World.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2232" data-file-width="3968" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Underwater_World.jpg/220px-Underwater_World.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Underwater_World.jpg/330px-Underwater_World.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Underwater_World.jpg/440px-Underwater_World.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Over 4,000 species of fish inhabit coral reefs.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coral_reef_PloS.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2141" data-file-width="1600" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Coral_reef_PloS.jpg/220px-Coral_reef_PloS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Coral_reef_PloS.jpg/330px-Coral_reef_PloS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Coral_reef_PloS.jpg/440px-Coral_reef_PloS.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Organisms can cover every square inch of a coral reef.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Coral reefs form some of the world's most productive ecosystems, providing complex and varied <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_habitat" title="Marine habitat">marine habitats</a> that support a wide range of other organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BarnesMann1991AquaticEcology_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-BarnesMann1991AquaticEcology-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuchs_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fuchs-84">[84]</a></sup><a href="/wiki/Fringing_reef" title="Fringing reef">Fringing reefs</a> just below <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_tide" title="Low tide">low tide</a> level have a mutually beneficial relationship with <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">mangrove</a> forests at high tide level and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_grass" title="Sea grass">sea grass</a> meadows in between: the reefs protect the mangroves and seagrass from strong currents and waves that would damage them or <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erode</a> the sediments in which they are rooted, while the mangroves and sea grass protect the coral from large influxes of <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a>, fresh water and <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollutants</a>. This level of variety in the environment benefits many coral reef animals, which, for example, may feed in the sea grass and use the reefs for protection or breeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reefs are home to a large variety of animals, including fish, <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabirds</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_sponge" title="Sea sponge">sponges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cnidaria" title="Cnidaria">cnidarians</a> (which includes some types of corals and <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a>), <a href="/wiki/Worm" title="Worm">worms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cleaner_shrimp" title="Cleaner shrimp">cleaner shrimp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spiny_lobster" title="Spiny lobster">spiny lobsters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crab" title="Crab">crabs</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusk" title="Mollusk">mollusks</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a>), <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">echinoderms</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Starfish" title="Starfish">starfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_urchin" title="Sea urchin">sea urchins</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holothuroidea" title="Holothuroidea">sea cucumbers</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_squirt" title="Sea squirt">sea squirts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">sea turtles</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_snake" title="Sea snake">sea snakes</a>. Aside from humans, <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> are rare on coral reefs, with visiting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetacean" title="Cetacean">cetaceans</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a> being the main exception. A few of these varied species feed directly on corals, while others graze on algae on the reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spalding_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Spalding-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Castro-56">[56]</a></sup> Reef biomass is positively related to species diversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>The same hideouts in a reef may be regularly inhabited by different species at different times of day. Nighttime predators such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardinalfish" title="Cardinalfish">cardinalfish</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squirrelfish" title="Squirrelfish">squirrelfish</a> hide during the day, while <a href="/wiki/Damselfish" title="Damselfish">damselfish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surgeonfish" title="Surgeonfish">surgeonfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triggerfish" title="Triggerfish">triggerfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wrasse" title="Wrasse">wrasses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parrotfish" title="Parrotfish">parrotfish</a> hide from <a href="/wiki/Eel" title="Eel">eels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murph_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-murph-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:49</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algae">Algae</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Algae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Reefs are chronically at risk of algal encroachment. Overfishing and excess nutrient supply from onshore can enable algae to outcompete and kill the coral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Increased nutrient levels can be a result of sewage or chemical fertilizer runoff from nearby coastal developments. Runoff can carry nitrogen and phosphorus which promote excess algae growth. Algae can sometimes out-compete the coral for space. The algae can then smother the coral by decreasing the oxygen supply available to the reef. Decreased oxygen levels can slow down coral's calcification rates weakening the coral and leaving it more susceptible to disease and degradation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> In surveys done around largely uninhabited US Pacific islands, algae inhabit a large percentage of surveyed coral locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The algal population consists of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turf_algae" title="Turf algae">turf algae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coralline_algae" title="Coralline algae">coralline algae</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macroalgae" title="Macroalgae">macro algae.</a></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sponges">Sponges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Sponges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sponges are essential for the functioning of the coral reef's ecosystem. Algae and corals in coral reefs produce organic material. This is filtered through sponges which convert this organic material into small particles which in turn are absorbed by algae and corals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fish">Fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef_fish" title="Coral reef fish">Coral reef fish</a></div>
<p>Over 4,000 species of fish inhabit coral reefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spalding_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Spalding-2">[2]</a></sup> The reasons for this diversity remain unclear. Hypotheses include the "lottery", in which the first (lucky winner) recruit to a territory is typically able to defend it against latecomers, "competition", in which adults compete for territory, and less-competitive species must be able to survive in poorer habitat, and "predation", in which population size is a function of postsettlement piscivore mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Healthy reefs can produce up to 35 tons of fish per square kilometer each year, but damaged reefs produce much less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McClellan_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-McClellan-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invertebrates">Invertebrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Invertebrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sea urchins, <a href="/wiki/Dotidae" title="Dotidae">Dotidae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nudibranch" title="Nudibranch">sea slugs</a> eat seaweed. Some species of sea urchins, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Diadema_antillarum" title="Diadema antillarum">Diadema antillarum</a></i>, can play a pivotal part in preventing algae from overrunning reefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nudibranchia" title="Nudibranchia">Nudibranchia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sea_anemone" title="Sea anemone">sea anemones</a> eat sponges.</p>
<p>A number of invertebrates, collectively called "cryptofauna," inhabit the coral skeletal substrate itself, either boring into the skeletons (through the process of <a href="/wiki/Bioerosion" title="Bioerosion">bioerosion</a>) or living in pre-existing voids and crevices. Those animals boring into the rock include sponges, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalve" title="Bivalve">bivalve</a> mollusks, and <a href="/wiki/Sipuncula" title="Sipuncula">sipunculans</a>. Those settling on the reef include many other species, particularly crustaceans and <a href="/wiki/Polychaete" title="Polychaete">polychaete</a> worms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nybakken_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nybakken-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seabirds">Seabirds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Seabirds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Coral reef systems provide important habitats for <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabird</a> species, some endangered. For example, <a href="/wiki/Midway_Atoll" title="Midway Atoll">Midway Atoll</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> supports nearly three million seabirds, including two-thirds (1.5 million) of the global population of <a href="/wiki/Laysan_albatross" title="Laysan albatross">Laysan albatross</a>, and one-third of the global population of <a href="/wiki/Black-footed_albatross" title="Black-footed albatross">black-footed albatross</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Each seabird species has specific sites on the atoll where they nest. Altogether, 17 species of seabirds live on Midway. The <a href="/wiki/Short-tailed_albatross" title="Short-tailed albatross">short-tailed albatross</a> is the rarest, with fewer than 2,200 surviving after excessive feather hunting in the late 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_snake" title="Sea snake">Sea snakes</a> feed exclusively on fish and their eggs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Marine birds, such as <a href="/wiki/Heron" title="Heron">herons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gannet" title="Gannet">gannets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pelican" title="Pelican">pelicans</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boobies" title="Boobies">boobies</a>, feed on reef fish. Some land-based <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> intermittently associate with reefs, such as <a href="/wiki/Monitor_lizard" title="Monitor lizard">monitor lizards</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crocodylus_porosus" title="Crocodylus porosus">marine crocodile</a> and semiaquatic snakes, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Laticauda_colubrina" title="Laticauda colubrina">Laticauda colubrina</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">Sea turtles</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Hawksbill_sea_turtle" title="Hawksbill sea turtle">hawksbill sea turtles</a>, feed on sponges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sea_turtle_loss_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sea_turtle_loss-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Importance">Importance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Importance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Coral reefs deliver <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a> to tourism, fisheries and coastline protection. The global economic value of coral reefs has been estimated to be between US $29.8 billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cesar_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cesar-6">[6]</a></sup> and $375 billion per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Costanza_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Costanza-7">[7]</a></sup> Coral reefs protect shorelines by absorbing wave energy, and many small islands would not exist without their reefs to protect them. According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_group" title="Environmental group">environmental group</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">World Wide Fund for Nature</a>, the economic cost over a 25-year period of destroying one kilometer of coral reef is somewhere between $137,000 and $1,200,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WWF_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-WWF-103">[103]</a></sup> About six million tons of fish are taken each year from coral reefs. Well-managed coral reefs have an annual yield of 15 tons of seafood on average per square kilometer. Southeast Asia's coral reef fisheries alone yield about $2.4 billion annually from seafood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WWF_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-WWF-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>To improve the management of coastal coral reefs, another environmental group, the <a href="/wiki/World_Resources_Institute" title="World Resources Institute">World Resources Institute</a> (WRI) developed and published tools for calculating the value of coral reef-related tourism, shoreline protection and fisheries, partnering with five Caribbean countries. As of April 2011, published working papers covered St. Lucia, Tobago, Belize, and the Dominican Republic, with a paper for Jamaica in preparation. The WRI was also "making sure that the study results support improved coastal policies and management planning".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The Belize study estimated the value of reef and mangrove services at $395–559 million annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bermuda's coral reefs provide economic benefits to the Island worth on average $722 million per year, based on six key ecosystem services, according to Sarkis <i>et al</i> (2010).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Threats">Threats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Threats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mvey0290.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1184" data-file-width="1808" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mvey0290.jpg/220px-Mvey0290.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mvey0290.jpg/330px-Mvey0290.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mvey0290.jpg/440px-Mvey0290.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mvey0290.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Island with <a href="/wiki/Fringing_reef" title="Fringing reef">fringing reef</a> off <a href="/wiki/Yap" title="Yap">Yap</a>, <a href="/wiki/Micronesia" title="Micronesia">Micronesia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coral_reefs_around_the_world_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coral_reefs_around_the_world-107">[107]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issues_with_coral_reefs" title="Environmental issues with coral reefs">Environmental issues with coral reefs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coral_bleaching" title="Coral bleaching">Coral bleaching</a></div>
<p>Coral reefs are dying around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coral_reefs_around_the_world_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coral_reefs_around_the_world-107">[107]</a></sup> In particular, coral mining, agricultural and urban runoff, <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> (organic and inorganic), <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blast_fishing" title="Blast fishing">blast fishing</a>, disease, and the digging of <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canals</a> and access into islands and bays are localized threats to coral ecosystems. Broader threats are sea temperature rise, sea level rise and <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> changes from <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>, all associated with <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions. A 2014 study lists factors such as population explosion along the coast lines, overfishing, the pollution of coastal areas, global warming and invasive species among the main reasons that have put reefs in danger of extinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>A study released in April 2013 has shown that air pollution can also stunt the growth of coral reefs; researchers from Australia, Panama and the UK used coral records (between 1880 and 2000) from the western Caribbean to show the threat of factors such as coal-burning coal and volcanic eruptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Pollutants, such as <a href="/wiki/Tributyltin" title="Tributyltin">Tributyltin</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Biocide" title="Biocide">biocide</a> released into water from in <a href="/wiki/Anti-fouling_paint" title="Anti-fouling paint">anti-fouling paint</a> can be toxic to corals.</p>
<p>In 2011, researchers suggested that "extant marine invertebrates face the same synergistic effects of multiple stressors" that occurred during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/End-Permian_extinction" title="End-Permian extinction">end-Permian extinction</a>, and that genera "with poorly buffered respiratory physiology and calcareous shells", such as corals, were particularly vulnerable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rock coral on seamounts across the ocean are under fire from <a href="/wiki/Bottom_trawling" title="Bottom trawling">bottom trawling</a>. Reportedly up to 50% of the catch is rock coral, and the practice transforms coral structures to rubble. With it taking years to regrow, these coral communities are disappearing faster than they can sustain themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another cause for the death of coral reefs is <a href="/wiki/Bioerosion" title="Bioerosion">bioerosion</a>. Various fishes graze corals, dead or alive and change the morphology of coral reefs making them more susceptible to other physical and chemical threats. It has been generally observed that only the algae growing on dead corals is eaten and the live ones are not. However, this act still destroys the top layer of coral substrate and makes it harder for the reefs to sustain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>In El Niño-year 2010, preliminary reports show global coral bleaching reached its worst level since another El Niño year, 1998, when 16% of the world's reefs died as a result of increased water temperature. In Indonesia's <a href="/wiki/Aceh" title="Aceh">Aceh</a> province, surveys showed some 80% of bleached corals died. Scientists do not yet understand the long-term impacts of coral bleaching, but they do know that bleaching leaves corals vulnerable to disease, stunts their growth, and affects their reproduction, while severe bleaching kills them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> In July, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> closed several dive sites where virtually all the corals were damaged by bleaching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ritter_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-ritter-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo2006_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo2006-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>To find answers for these problems, researchers study the various factors that impact reefs. The list includes the ocean's role as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_sink" title="Carbon dioxide sink">carbon dioxide sink</a>, atmospheric changes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_light" title="Ultraviolet light">ultraviolet light</a>, ocean acidification, <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>, impacts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dust_storms" title="Dust storms">dust storms</a> carrying agents to far-flung reefs, pollutants, <a href="/wiki/Algal_bloom" title="Algal bloom">algal blooms</a> and others. Reefs are threatened well beyond coastal areas.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Coral reefs with one type of zooxanthellae are more prone to bleaching than are reefs with another, more hardy, species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>General estimates show approximately 10% of the world's coral reefs are dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleypas.2C_J.A._2006_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kleypas.2C_J.A._2006-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> About 60% of the world's reefs are at risk due to destructive, human-related activities. The threat to the health of reefs is particularly high in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, where 95% of reefs are at risk from local threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> By the 2030s, 90% of reefs are expected to be at risk from both human activities and <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>; by 2050, all coral reefs will be in danger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Current research is showing that ecotourism in the Great Barrier Reef is contributing to coral disease,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> and that chemicals in sunscreens may contribute to the impact of viruses on zooxanthellae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sunscreen_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sunscreen-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some scientists, including those associated with the <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a>, posit that US coral reefs are likely to disappear within a few decades as a result of global warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Protection">Protection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Protection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef_protection" title="Coral reef protection">Coral reef protection</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg/220px-Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg/330px-Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg/440px-Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A diversity of corals</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_Protected_Area" title="Marine Protected Area">Marine protected areas</a> (MPAs) have become increasingly prominent for reef management. MPAs promote responsible <a href="/wiki/Fisheries_management" title="Fisheries management">fishery management</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitat_(ecology)" title="Habitat (ecology)">habitat protection</a>. Much like <a href="/wiki/National_park" title="National park">national parks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_refuge" title="Wildlife refuge">wildlife refuges</a>, and to varying degrees, MPAs restrict potentially damaging activities. MPAs encompass both social and biological objectives, including reef restoration, aesthetics, biodiversity, and economic benefits. However, there are very few MPAs that have actually made a substantial difference. Research in Indonesia, Philippines and Papua New Guinea shows that there is no significant difference between an MPA site and an unprotected site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Conflicts surrounding MPAs involve lack of participation, clashing views of the government and fisheries, effectiveness of the area, and funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> In some situations, as in the <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_Islands_Protected_Area" title="Phoenix Islands Protected Area">Phoenix Islands Protected Area</a>, MPAs can also provide revenue, potentially equal to the income they would have generated without controls, as <a href="/wiki/Kiribati" title="Kiribati">Kiribati</a> did for its <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_Islands" title="Phoenix Islands">Phoenix Islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <i><a class="external text" href="http://cmsdata.iucn.org/downloads/caribbean_coral_reefs___status_report_1970_2012.pdf" rel="nofollow">Caribbean Coral Reefs - Status Report 1970-2012</a></i> made by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">IUCN</a>. States that; stopping <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a> especially <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef_fish#Herbivores" title="Coral reef fish">key fishes to coral reef</a> like <a href="/wiki/Parrotfish#Economic_importance" title="Parrotfish">parrotfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Integrated_coastal_zone_management#The_importance_of_the_Coastal_Zone_and_the_need_for_management" title="Integrated coastal zone management">coastal zone management</a> which reduce human pressure on reef, (for example restricting the coastal settlement, development and tourism in coastal reef) and controlling pollution specially <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage wastage</a>, may not only reduce coral declining but also reverse it and may let to coral reef more adaptable to changes relates to climate and acidification. The report shows that healthier reef in the Caribbean are those with large population of parrotfish in countries which protect these key fishes and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_urchins" title="Sea urchins">sea urchins</a>, banning <a href="/wiki/Fish_trap" title="Fish trap">fish trap</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spearfishing" title="Spearfishing">Spearfishing</a> creating "resilient reefs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCNvideo_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCNvideo-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>To help combat ocean acidification, some laws are in place to reduce greenhouse gases such as carbon dioxide. The Clean Water Act puts pressure on state government agencies to monitor and limit runoff of pollutants that can cause ocean acidification. Stormwater surge preventions are also in place, as well as coastal buffers between agricultural land and the coastline. This act also ensures that delicate watershed ecosystems are intact, such as wetlands. The Clean Water Act is funded by the federal government, and is monitored by various watershed groups. Many land use laws aim to reduce CO<sub>2</sub> emissions by limiting deforestation. Deforestation causes erosion, which releases a large amount of carbon stored in the soil, which then flows into the ocean, contributing to ocean acidification. Incentives are used to reduce miles traveled by vehicles, which reduces the carbon emissions into the atmosphere, thereby reducing the amount of dissolved CO<sub>2</sub> in the ocean. State and federal governments also control coastal erosion, which releases stored carbon in the soil into the ocean, increasing ocean acidification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> High-end satellite technology is increasingly being employed to monitor coral reef conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biosphere_reserve" title="Biosphere reserve">Biosphere reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marine_park" title="Marine park">marine park</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_monument" title="National monument">national monument</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_heritage" title="World heritage">world heritage</a> status can protect reefs. For example, <a href="/wiki/Belize_Barrier_Reef" title="Belize Barrier Reef">Belize's barrier reef</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sian_Ka%27an" title="Sian Ka'an">Sian Ka'an</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galapagos" title="Galapagos">Galapagos</a> islands, <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Great Barrier Reef">Great Barrier Reef</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henderson_Island_(Pitcairn_Islands)" title="Henderson Island (Pitcairn Islands)">Henderson Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palau" title="Palau">Palau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Papah%C4%81naumoku%C4%81kea_Marine_National_Monument" title="Papahānaumokuākea Marine National Monument">Papahānaumokuākea Marine National Monument</a> are world heritage sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Australia, the Great Barrier Reef is protected by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef_Marine_Park_Authority" title="Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority">Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority</a>, and is the subject of much legislation, including a biodiversity action plan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBRMPA_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-GBRMPA-133">[133]</a></sup> They have compiled a Coral Reef Resilience Action Plan. This detailed action plan consists of numerous <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_management" title="Adaptive management">adaptive management</a> strategies, including reducing our carbon footprint, which would ultimately reduce the amount of ocean acidification in the oceans surrounding the Great Barrier Reef. An extensive public awareness plan is also in place to provide education on the “rainforests of the sea” and how people can reduce carbon emissions, thereby reducing ocean acidification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Great_Barrier_Reef_Marine_Park_Authority_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Great_Barrier_Reef_Marine_Park_Authority-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>Inhabitants of Ahus Island, <a href="/wiki/Manus_Province" title="Manus Province">Manus Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a>, have followed a generations-old practice of restricting fishing in six areas of their reef lagoon. Their cultural traditions allow line fishing, but no net or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spear_fishing" title="Spear fishing">spear fishing</a>. The result is both the <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> and individual fish sizes are significantly larger than in places where fishing is unrestricted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Restoration">Restoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Restoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coral_planting_and_reef_restoration_may_11.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Coral_planting_and_reef_restoration_may_11.jpg/220px-Coral_planting_and_reef_restoration_may_11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Coral_planting_and_reef_restoration_may_11.jpg/330px-Coral_planting_and_reef_restoration_may_11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Coral_planting_and_reef_restoration_may_11.jpg/440px-Coral_planting_and_reef_restoration_may_11.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coral_planting_and_reef_restoration_may_11.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coral fragments growing on nontoxic concrete</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture_of_coral" title="Aquaculture of coral">Aquaculture of coral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artificial_reef" title="Artificial reef">Artificial reef</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Restoration_ecology" title="Restoration ecology">Restoration ecology</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coral_aquaculture" title="Coral aquaculture">Coral aquaculture</a>, also known as coral farming or coral gardening, is showing promise as a potentially effective tool for restoring coral reefs, which have been declining around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horoszowski-Fridman_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horoszowski-Fridman-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pomeroy_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pomeroy-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rinkevich_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rinkevich-139">[139]</a></sup> The process bypasses the early growth stages of corals when they are most at risk of dying. Coral seeds are grown in nurseries, then replanted on the reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferse_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferse-140">[140]</a></sup> Coral is farmed by coral farmers who live locally to the reefs and farm for reef <a href="/wiki/Conservation_movement" title="Conservation movement">conservation</a> or for income.</p>
<p>Efforts to expand the size and number of coral reefs generally involve supplying substrate to allow more corals to find a home. Substrate materials include discarded vehicle tires, scuttled ships, subway cars, and formed concrete, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reef_Ball" title="Reef Ball">reef balls</a>. Reefs also grow unaided on marine structures such as <a href="/wiki/Oil_platform" title="Oil platform">oil rigs</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In large restoration projects, propagated <a href="/wiki/Hermatypic_coral" title="Hermatypic coral">hermatypic coral</a> on substrate can be secured with metal pins, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superglue" title="Superglue">superglue</a> or <a href="/wiki/Milliput" title="Milliput">milliput</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Needle and thread can also attach A-hermatype coral to substrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>A substrate for growing corals referred to as <a href="/wiki/Biorock" title="Biorock">Biorock</a> is produced by running low voltage <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_current" title="Electrical current">electrical currents</a> through seawater to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystallize" title="Crystallize">crystallize</a> dissolved minerals onto <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> structures. The resultant white carbonate (<a href="/wiki/Aragonite" title="Aragonite">aragonite</a>) is the same mineral that makes up natural coral reefs. Corals rapidly colonize and grow at accelerated rates on these coated structures. The electrical currents also accelerate formation and growth of both chemical limestone rock and the skeletons of corals and other shell-bearing organisms. The vicinity of the <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anode</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">cathode</a> provides a high-<a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> environment which inhibits the growth of competitive filamentous and fleshy algae. The increased growth rates fully depend on the accretion activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sabater_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sabater-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>During accretion, the settled corals display an increased growth rate, size and density, but after the process is complete, growth rate and density return to levels comparable to natural growth, and are about the same size or slightly smaller.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sabater_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sabater-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>One case study with coral reef restoration was conducted on the island of Oahu in Hawaii. The University of Hawaii has come up with a Coral Reef Assessment and Monitoring Program to help relocate and restore coral reefs in Hawaii. A boat channel on the island of Oahu to the Hawaii Institute of Marine Biology was overcrowded with coral reefs. Also, many areas of coral reef patches in the channel had been damaged from past dredging in the channel. Dredging covers the existing corals with sand, and their larvae cannot build and thrive on sand; they can only build on to existing reefs. Because of this, the University of Hawaii decided to relocate some of the coral reef to a different transplant site. They transplanted them with the help of the United States Army divers, to a relocation site relatively close to the channel. They observed very little, if any, damage occurred to any of the colonies while they were being transported, and no mortality of coral reefs has been observed on the new transplant site, but they will be continuing to monitor the new transplant site to see how potential environmental impacts (i.e. ocean acidification) will harm the overall reef mortality rate. While trying to attach the coral to the new transplant site, they found the coral placed on hard rock is growing considerably well, and coral was even growing on the wires that attached the transplant corals to the transplant site. This gives new hope to future research on coral reef transplant sites. As a result of this coral restoration project, no environmental effects were seen from the transplantation process, no recreational activities were decreased, and no scenic areas were affected by the project. This is a great example that coral transplantation and restoration can work and thrive under the right conditions, which means there may be hope for other damaged coral reefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jokeil_P.L..2C_Ku.E2.80.99ulei_S.R._144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jokeil_P.L..2C_Ku.E2.80.99ulei_S.R.-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another possibility for coral restoration is gene therapy. Through infecting coral with genetically modified bacteria, it may be possible to grow corals that are more resistant to climate change and other threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reefs_in_the_past">Reefs in the past</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coral_reef&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Reefs in the past">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ancient_coral_reefs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="655" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ancient_coral_reefs.jpg/260px-Ancient_coral_reefs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ancient_coral_reefs.jpg/390px-Ancient_coral_reefs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ancient_coral_reefs.jpg/520px-Ancient_coral_reefs.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ancient_coral_reefs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ancient coral reefs</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Throughout <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> history, from a few thousand years after hard skeletons were developed by marine organisms, there were almost always reefs. The times of maximum development were in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Cambrian" title="Middle Cambrian">Middle Cambrian</a> (513–501 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annum" title="Annum">Ma</a>), <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> (416–359 Ma) and <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> (359–299 Ma), owing to order <a href="/wiki/Rugosa" title="Rugosa">Rugosa</a> <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinct</a> corals, and <a href="/wiki/Late_Cretaceous" title="Late Cretaceous">Late Cretaceous</a> (100–66 Ma) and all <a href="/wiki/Neogene" title="Neogene">Neogene</a> (23 Ma–present), owing to order <a href="/wiki/Scleractinia" title="Scleractinia">Scleractinia</a> corals.</p>
<p>Not all reefs in the past were formed by corals: those in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Cambrian" title="Early Cambrian">Early Cambrian</a> (542–513 Ma) resulted from calcareous <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archaeocyatha" title="Archaeocyatha">archaeocyathids</a> (small animals with conical shape, probably related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sponges" title="Sponges">sponges</a>) and in the <a href="/wiki/Late_Cretaceous" title="Late Cretaceous">Late Cretaceous</a> (100–66 Ma), when there also existed reefs formed by a group of <a href="/wiki/Bivalvia" title="Bivalvia">bivalves</a> called <a href="/wiki/Rudists" title="Rudists">rudists</a>; one of the valves formed the main conical structure and the other, much smaller valve acted as a cap.</p>
<p>Measurements of the oxygen isotopic composition of the aragonitic skeleton of coral reefs, such as <a href="/wiki/Porites" title="Porites">Porites</a>, can indicate changes in the sea surface temperature and sea surface salinity conditions of the ocean during the growth of the coral. This technique is often used by climate scientists to infer the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleoclimate" title="Paleoclimate">paleoclimate</a> of a region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cobb_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cobb-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
